<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Retail</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Sale of goods and services from individuals or businesses to the end-user</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For the comic strip by Norm Feuti, see <a href="/wiki/Retail_(comic_strip)" title="Retail (comic strip)">Retail (comic strip)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Comercio_en_la_plaza_del_9_de_abril_de_1947,_T%C3%A1nger,_Marruecos,_2015-12-11,_DD_78.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5687" data-file-width="4335" decoding="async" height="289" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Comercio_en_la_plaza_del_9_de_abril_de_1947%2C_T%C3%A1nger%2C_Marruecos%2C_2015-12-11%2C_DD_78.JPG/220px-Comercio_en_la_plaza_del_9_de_abril_de_1947%2C_T%C3%A1nger%2C_Marruecos%2C_2015-12-11%2C_DD_78.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Comercio_en_la_plaza_del_9_de_abril_de_1947%2C_T%C3%A1nger%2C_Marruecos%2C_2015-12-11%2C_DD_78.JPG/330px-Comercio_en_la_plaza_del_9_de_abril_de_1947%2C_T%C3%A1nger%2C_Marruecos%2C_2015-12-11%2C_DD_78.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Comercio_en_la_plaza_del_9_de_abril_de_1947%2C_T%C3%A1nger%2C_Marruecos%2C_2015-12-11%2C_DD_78.JPG/440px-Comercio_en_la_plaza_del_9_de_abril_de_1947%2C_T%C3%A1nger%2C_Marruecos%2C_2015-12-11%2C_DD_78.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Comercio_en_la_plaza_del_9_de_abril_de_1947,_T%C3%A1nger,_Marruecos,_2015-12-11,_DD_78.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A grocery and cosmetics store in <a href="/wiki/Tangier" title="Tangier">Tangier</a>, Morocco</div></div></div>
<p><b>Retail</b> is the sale of <a href="/wiki/Goods" title="Goods">goods</a> and <a href="/wiki/Service_(economics)" title="Service (economics)">services</a> to <a href="/wiki/Consumer" title="Consumer">consumers</a>, in contrast to <a href="/wiki/Wholesaling" title="Wholesaling">wholesaling</a>, which is sale to business or institutional customers. A <b>retailer</b> purchases goods in large quantities from <a href="/wiki/Manufacturing" title="Manufacturing">manufacturers</a>, directly or through a wholesaler, and then sells in smaller quantities to consumers for a <a href="/wiki/Profit_(accounting)" title="Profit (accounting)">profit</a>. Retailers are the final link in the <a href="/wiki/Supply_chain" title="Supply chain">supply chain</a> from producers to consumers.
</p><p>Retail markets and shops have a very ancient history, dating back to antiquity. Some of the earliest retailers were itinerant <a href="/wiki/Peddler" title="Peddler">peddlers</a>. Over the centuries, retail shops were transformed from little more than "rude booths" to the sophisticated shopping malls of the modern era. In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Digital_age" title="Digital age">digital age</a>, an increasing number of retailers are seeking to reach broader markets by selling through multiple channels, including both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bricks_and_mortar_store" title="Bricks and mortar store">bricks and mortar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Online_shopping" title="Online shopping">online retailing</a>. Digital technologies are also affecting the way that <a href="/wiki/Consumer" title="Consumer">consumers</a> pay for goods and services. Retailing support services may also include the provision of credit, delivery services, advisory services, stylist services and a range of other supporting services.
</p><p>Most modern retailers typically make a variety of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corporate_strategy" title="Corporate strategy">strategic level</a> decisions including the type of store, the <a href="/wiki/Market_(economics)" title="Market (economics)">market</a> to be served, the optimal <a href="/wiki/Product_(business)" title="Product (business)">product</a> assortment, <a href="/wiki/Customer_service" title="Customer service">customer service</a>, supporting services, and the store's overall market positioning. Once the strategic retail plan is in place, retailers devise the retail mix which includes product, price, place, promotion, personnel, and presentation.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Retail&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The word <i>retail</i> comes from the <a href="/wiki/Old_French" title="Old French">Old French</a> verb <i>tailler</i>, meaning "to cut off, clip, pare, divide in terms of tailoring" (c. 1365). It was first recorded as a noun in 1433 with the meaning of "a sale in small quantities" from the <a href="/wiki/Middle_French" title="Middle French">Middle French</a> verb <i>retailler</i> meaning "a piece cut off, shred, scrap, paring".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> At the present, the meaning of the word <i>retail</i> (in English, French, Dutch, German and Spanish) refers to the sale of small quantities of items to consumers (as opposed to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wholesale" title="Wholesale">wholesale</a>).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition_and_explanation">Definition and explanation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Retail&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Definition and explanation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Retail refers to the activity of selling goods or services directly to consumers or end-users.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> Some retailers may sell to business customers, and such sales are termed <i>non-retail activity.</i> In some jurisdictions or regions, legal definitions of retail specify that at least 80 percent of sales activity must be to end-users.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Retailing often occurs in retail stores or service establishments, but may also occur through direct selling such as through vending machines, door-to-door sales or electronic channels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>
Although the idea of retail is often associated with the purchase of goods, the term may be applied to service providers that sell to consumers. Retail service providers include retail banking, tourism, insurance, private healthcare, private education, private security firms, legal firms, publishers, public transport, and others. For example, a tourism provider might have a retail division that books travel and accommodation for consumers plus a wholesale division that purchases blocks of accommodation, hospitality, transport, and sightseeing which are subsequently packaged into a holiday tour for sale to retail travel agents.
</p><p>Some retailers badge their stores as "wholesale outlets" offering "wholesale prices." While this practice may encourage consumers to imagine that they have access to lower prices, while being prepared to trade-off reduced prices for cramped in-store environments, in a strictly legal sense, a store that sells the majority of its merchandise direct to consumers, is defined as a retailer rather than a wholesaler. Different jurisdictions set parameters for the ratio of consumer to business sales that define a retail business.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Retail&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_retail" title="History of retail">History of retail</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mercati_di_Traiano_-_Roma.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5355" data-file-width="8384" decoding="async" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Mercati_di_Traiano_-_Roma.jpg/220px-Mercati_di_Traiano_-_Roma.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Mercati_di_Traiano_-_Roma.jpg/330px-Mercati_di_Traiano_-_Roma.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Mercati_di_Traiano_-_Roma.jpg/440px-Mercati_di_Traiano_-_Roma.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mercati_di_Traiano_-_Roma.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Marketplace at Trajan's Forum, the earliest known example of permanent retail shopfronts</div></div></div><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2013-01-02_Grand_Bazaar,_Istanbul_04.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1800" data-file-width="2700" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/2013-01-02_Grand_Bazaar%2C_Istanbul_04.jpg/220px-2013-01-02_Grand_Bazaar%2C_Istanbul_04.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/2013-01-02_Grand_Bazaar%2C_Istanbul_04.jpg/330px-2013-01-02_Grand_Bazaar%2C_Istanbul_04.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/2013-01-02_Grand_Bazaar%2C_Istanbul_04.jpg/440px-2013-01-02_Grand_Bazaar%2C_Istanbul_04.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2013-01-02_Grand_Bazaar,_Istanbul_04.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Grand Bazaar, Istanbul (interior). Established in 1455, it is thought to be the oldest continuously operating covered market</div></div></div><p>Retail markets have existed since ancient times. Archaeological evidence for trade, probably involving barter systems, dates back more than 10,000 years. As civilizations grew, barter was replaced with retail trade involving coinage. Selling and buying are thought to have emerged in Asia Minor (modern Turkey) in around the 7th-millennium BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">ancient Greece</a>, markets operated within the <a href="/wiki/Agora" title="Agora">agora</a>, an open space where, on market days, goods were displayed on mats or temporary stalls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">ancient Rome</a>, trade took place in the <a href="/wiki/Forum_(Roman)" title="Forum (Roman)">forum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> The Roman forum was arguably the earliest example of a permanent retail shop-front.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Recent research suggests that China exhibited a rich history of early retail systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> From as early as 200 BCE, Chinese packaging and branding were used to signal family, place names and product quality, and the use of government imposed product branding was used between 600 and 900 CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Eckhart and Bengtsson have argued that during the Song Dynasty (960–1127), Chinese society developed a consumerist culture, where a high level of consumption was attainable for a wide variety of ordinary consumers rather than just the elite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
In <a href="/wiki/England_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="England in the Middle Ages">Medieval England</a> and <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, relatively few permanent shops were to be found; instead, customers walked into the tradesman's workshops where they discussed purchasing options directly with tradesmen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> In the more populous cities, a small number of shops were beginning to emerge by the 13th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Outside the major cities, most consumable purchases were made through markets or fairs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Market-places appear to have emerged independently outside Europe. The <a href="/wiki/Grand_Bazaar,_Istanbul" title="Grand Bazaar, Istanbul">Grand Bazaar</a> in <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a> is often cited as the world's oldest continuously operating market; its construction began in 1455. The Spanish conquistadors wrote glowingly of markets in the Americas. In the 15th century, the <a href="/wiki/Mexica" title="Mexica">Mexica</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aztec" title="Aztec">Aztec</a>) market of <a href="/wiki/Tlatelolco_(altepetl)" title="Tlatelolco (altepetl)">Tlatelolco</a> was the largest in all the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ana_Marie_Wirth_Apotheke.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="762" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Ana_Marie_Wirth_Apotheke.jpg/220px-Ana_Marie_Wirth_Apotheke.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Ana_Marie_Wirth_Apotheke.jpg/330px-Ana_Marie_Wirth_Apotheke.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Ana_Marie_Wirth_Apotheke.jpg/440px-Ana_Marie_Wirth_Apotheke.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ana_Marie_Wirth_Apotheke.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The retail service counter was an innovation of the eighteenth century</div></div></div>
<p>By the 17th century, permanent shops with more regular trading hours were beginning to supplant markets and fairs as the main retail outlet. Provincial shopkeepers were active in almost every English market town.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> As the number of shops grew, they underwent a transformation. The trappings of a modern shop, which had been entirely absent from the sixteenth- and early seventeenth-century store, gradually made way for store interiors and shopfronts that are more familiar to modern shoppers. Prior to the eighteenth century, the typical retail store had no counter, display cases, chairs, mirrors, changing rooms, etc. However, the opportunity for the customer to browse merchandise, touch and feel products began to be available, with retail innovations from the late 17th and early 18th centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galeries_de_bois_(ancien_camp_des_Tartares),_au_Palais-Royal,_1825.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3058" data-file-width="5175" decoding="async" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Galeries_de_bois_%28ancien_camp_des_Tartares%29%2C_au_Palais-Royal%2C_1825.jpg/220px-Galeries_de_bois_%28ancien_camp_des_Tartares%29%2C_au_Palais-Royal%2C_1825.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Galeries_de_bois_%28ancien_camp_des_Tartares%29%2C_au_Palais-Royal%2C_1825.jpg/330px-Galeries_de_bois_%28ancien_camp_des_Tartares%29%2C_au_Palais-Royal%2C_1825.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Galeries_de_bois_%28ancien_camp_des_Tartares%29%2C_au_Palais-Royal%2C_1825.jpg/440px-Galeries_de_bois_%28ancien_camp_des_Tartares%29%2C_au_Palais-Royal%2C_1825.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galeries_de_bois_(ancien_camp_des_Tartares),_au_Palais-Royal,_1825.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Galeries de bois at au Palais-Royal, one of the earliest shopping arcades in Europe</div></div></div><p>
By the late 18th century, grand shopping arcades began to emerge across Europe and in the Antipodes. A shopping arcade refers to a multiple-vendor space, operating under a covered roof. Typically, the roof was constructed of glass to allow for natural light and to reduce the need for candles or electric lighting. Some of the earliest examples of shopping arcade appeared in Paris, due to its lack of pavement for pedestrians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Conlin,_J._2013_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Conlin,_J._2013-18">[18]</a></sup> While the arcades were the province of the bourgeoisie, a new type of retail venture emerged to serve the needs of the working poor. <a href="/wiki/John_Stuart_Mill" title="John Stuart Mill">John Stuart Mill</a> wrote about the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Consumers%27_co-operative" title="Consumers' co-operative">co-operative</a> retail store, which he witnessed first-hand in the mid-nineteenth century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AA446b_copy.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="553" data-file-width="864" decoding="async" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/AA446b_copy.jpeg/220px-AA446b_copy.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/AA446b_copy.jpeg/330px-AA446b_copy.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/AA446b_copy.jpeg/440px-AA446b_copy.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AA446b_copy.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Department stores, such as <a href="/wiki/Le_Bon_March%C3%A9" title="Le Bon Marché">Le Bon Marché</a> of France, appeared from the mid nineteenth century</div></div></div>
<p>The modern era of retailing is defined as the period from the industrial revolution to the 21st century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> In major cities, the <a href="/wiki/Department_store" title="Department store">department store</a> emerged in the mid- to late 19th century, and permanently reshaped shopping habits, and redefined concepts of service and luxury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Many of the early department stores were more than just a retail emporium; rather they were venues where shoppers could spend their leisure time and be entertained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Retail, using mail order, came of age during the mid-19th century. Although catalogue sales had been used since the 15th century, this method of retailing was confined to a few industries such as the sale of books and seeds. However, improvements in transport and postal services led several entrepreneurs on either side of the Atlantic to experiment with catalogue sales.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the post-war period, an American architect, <a href="/wiki/Victor_Gruen" title="Victor Gruen">Victor Gruen</a> developed a concept for a shopping mall; a planned, self-contained shopping complex complete with an indoor plaza, statues, planting schemes, piped music, and car-parking. Gruen's vision was to create a shopping atmosphere where people felt so comfortable, they would spend more time in the environment, thereby enhancing opportunities for purchasing. The first of these malls opened at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northland_Center_(Michigan)" title="Northland Center (Michigan)">Northland Mall</a> near Detroit in 1954.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYer04_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYer04-24">[24]</a></sup> Throughout the twentieth century, a trend towards larger store footprints became discernible. The average size of a U.S. supermarket grew from 31,000 square feet (2,900 m<sup>2</sup>) square feet in 1991 to 44,000 square feet (4,100 m<sup>2</sup>) square feet in 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> By the end of the twentieth century, stores were using labels such as "mega-stores" and "warehouse" stores to reflect their growing size.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> The upward trend of increasing retail space was not consistent across nations and led in the early 21st century to a 2-fold difference in square footage per capita between the United States and Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>As the 21st century takes shape, some indications suggest that large retail stores have come under increasing pressure from online sales models and that reductions in store size are evident.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Under such competition and other issues such as business debt,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> there has been a noted business disruption called the <a href="/wiki/Retail_apocalypse" title="Retail apocalypse">retail apocalypse</a> in recent years which several retail businesses, especially in North America, are sharply reducing their number of stores, or going out of business entirely.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Retail_strategy">Retail strategy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Retail&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Retail strategy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Strategic_planning" title="Strategic planning">Strategic planning</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Supermarket_check_out.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="972" data-file-width="1296" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Supermarket_check_out.JPG/220px-Supermarket_check_out.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Supermarket_check_out.JPG/330px-Supermarket_check_out.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Supermarket_check_out.JPG/440px-Supermarket_check_out.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Supermarket_check_out.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Retailers make many strategic decisions – store type, market served, product assortment and customer services</div></div></div>
<p>The distinction between "strategic" and "managerial" decision-making is commonly used to distinguish "two phases having different goals and based on different conceptual tools. Strategic planning concerns the choice of policies aiming at improving the competitive position of the firm, taking account of challenges and opportunities proposed by the competitive environment. On the other hand, managerial decision-making is focused on the implementation of specific targets."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>In retailing, the strategic plan is designed to set out the <a href="/wiki/Vision_statement" title="Vision statement">vision</a> and provide guidance for retail decision-makers and provide an outline of how the product and service mix will optimize customer satisfaction. As part of the strategic planning process, it is customary for strategic planners to carry out a detailed environmental scan which seeks to identify trends and opportunities in the competitive environment, market environment, economic environment and statutory-political environment. The retail strategy is normally devised or reviewed every 3– 5 years by the chief executive officer. The profit margins of retailers depend largely on their ability to achieve market competitive transaction costs.
</p><p>The strategic retail analysis typically includes following elements:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lanvin_Monte_Carlo_2004.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1697" data-file-width="2357" decoding="async" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Lanvin_Monte_Carlo_2004.jpg/220px-Lanvin_Monte_Carlo_2004.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Lanvin_Monte_Carlo_2004.jpg/330px-Lanvin_Monte_Carlo_2004.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Lanvin_Monte_Carlo_2004.jpg/440px-Lanvin_Monte_Carlo_2004.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lanvin_Monte_Carlo_2004.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The retailer also considers the overall strategic position and retail image</div></div></div>
<ul><li><b>Market analysis</b> – Market size, stage of market, market competitiveness, market attractiveness, market trends</li>
<li><b>Customer analysis</b> – <a href="/wiki/Market_segmentation" title="Market segmentation">Market segmentation</a>, demographic, geographic and psychographic profile, values and attitudes, shopping habits, brand preferences, analysis of needs and wants, media habits</li>
<li><b>Internal analysis</b> – Other capabilities e.g. human resource capability, technological capability, financial capability, ability to generate <a href="/wiki/Economies_of_scale" title="Economies of scale">scale economies</a> or <a href="/wiki/Economies_of_scope" title="Economies of scope">economies of scope</a>, trade relations, reputation, positioning, past performance</li>
<li><b>Competition analysis</b> – Availability of substitutes, competitor's strengths and weaknesses, perceptual mapping, competitive trends</li>
<li><b>Review of product mix</b> – :: Sales per square foot, stock-turnover rates, profitability per product line</li>
<li><b>Review of distribution channels</b> – Lead-times between placing order and delivery, cost of distribution, cost efficiency of intermediaries</li>
<li><b>Evaluation of the economics of the strategy</b> – Cost-benefit analysis of planned activities</li></ul>
<p>At the conclusion of the retail analysis, retail marketers should have a clear idea of which groups of customers are to be the target of marketing activities. Not all elements are, however, equal, often with demographics, shopping motivations, and spending directing consumer activities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Retail research studies suggest that there is a strong relationship between a store's positioning and the socio-economic status of customers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> In addition, the retail strategy, including service quality, has a significant and positive association with customer loyalty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> A marketing strategy effectively outlines all key aspects of firms' targeted audience, demographics, preferences. In a highly competitive market, the retail strategy sets up long-term sustainability. It focuses on customer relationships, stressing the importance of added value, customer satisfaction and highlights how the store's market positioning appeals to targeted groups of customers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Retail_marketing">Retail marketing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Retail&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Retail marketing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Retail_marketing" title="Retail marketing">Retail marketing</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Retail_mix.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="737" data-file-width="945" decoding="async" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Retail_mix.jpg/260px-Retail_mix.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Retail_mix.jpg/390px-Retail_mix.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Retail_mix.jpg/520px-Retail_mix.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Retail_mix.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The retail marketing mix or the 6 Ps of retailing</div></div></div><p>
A retail mix is devised for the purpose of coordinating day-to-day tactical decisions. The retail marketing mix typically consists of six broad decision layers including product decisions, place decisions, promotion, price, personnel and presentation (also known as physical evidence). The retail mix is loosely based on the <a href="/wiki/Marketing_mix" title="Marketing mix">marketing mix</a>, but has been expanded and modified in line with the unique needs of the retail context. A number of scholars have argued for an expanded marketing, mix with the inclusion of two new Ps, namely, <i>Personnel</i> and <i>Presentation</i> since these contribute to the customer's unique retail experience and are the principal basis for retail differentiation. Yet other scholars argue that the <i>Retail Format</i> (i.e. retail formula) should be included.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> The modified retail marketing mix that is most commonly cited in textbooks is often called the <i>6 Ps of retailing</i> (see diagram at right).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Supermarket_full_of_goods.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3995" data-file-width="5640" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Supermarket_full_of_goods.jpg/220px-Supermarket_full_of_goods.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Supermarket_full_of_goods.jpg/330px-Supermarket_full_of_goods.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Supermarket_full_of_goods.jpg/440px-Supermarket_full_of_goods.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Supermarket_full_of_goods.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A typical supermarket carries an assortment of between 30,000 and 60,000 different products</div></div></div><p>The primary <a href="/wiki/Product_management" title="Product management">product-related decisions</a> facing the retailer are the product assortment (what product lines, how many lines and which brands to carry); the type of customer service (high contact through to self-service) and the availability of support services (e.g. credit terms, delivery services, after sales care). These decisions depend on careful analysis of the market, demand, competition as well as the retailer's skills and expertise.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Customer_service" title="Customer service">Customer service</a> is the "sum of acts and elements that allow consumers to receive what they need or desire from [the] retail establishment." Retailers must decide whether to provide a full service outlet or minimal service outlet, such as no-service in the case of vending machines; self-service with only basic sales assistance or a full service operation as in many boutiques and speciality stores. In addition, the retailer needs to make decisions about sales support such as customer delivery and after sales customer care.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Souvenir.london.arp.750pix.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="536" data-file-width="750" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Souvenir.london.arp.750pix.jpg/220px-Souvenir.london.arp.750pix.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Souvenir.london.arp.750pix.jpg/330px-Souvenir.london.arp.750pix.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Souvenir.london.arp.750pix.jpg/440px-Souvenir.london.arp.750pix.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Souvenir.london.arp.750pix.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sellers of souvenirs are typically located in high traffic areas such as this London souvenir stand situated near a railway station on a busy street corner</div></div></div><p><a href="/wiki/Site_selection" title="Site selection">Place decisions</a> are primarily concerned with consumer access and may involve location, space utilisation and operating hours. Retailers may consider a range of both qualitative and quantitative factors to evaluate to potential sites under consideration. Macro factors include market characteristics (demographic, economic and socio-cultural), demand, competition and infrastructure (e.g. the availability of power, roads, public transport systems).  Micro factors include the size of the site (e.g. availability of parking), access for delivery vehicles. A major retail trend has been the shift to multi-channel retailing. To counter the disruption caused by online retail, many bricks and mortar retailers have entered the online retail space, by setting up online catalogue sales and <a href="/wiki/E-commerce" title="E-commerce">e-commerce</a> websites. However, many retailers have noticed that consumers behave differently when shopping online. For instance, in terms of choice of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Online_marketing_platform" title="Online marketing platform">online platform</a>, shoppers tend to choose the online site of their preferred retailer initially, but as they gain more experience in online shopping, they become less loyal and more likely to switch to other retail sites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Online_shopping" title="Online shopping">Online stores</a> are usually available 24 hours a day, and many consumers across the globe have Internet access both at work and at home.
</p><div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fan-Tankstelle_von_Dynamo_Dresden.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1280" data-file-width="960" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Fan-Tankstelle_von_Dynamo_Dresden.JPG/170px-Fan-Tankstelle_von_Dynamo_Dresden.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Fan-Tankstelle_von_Dynamo_Dresden.JPG/255px-Fan-Tankstelle_von_Dynamo_Dresden.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Fan-Tankstelle_von_Dynamo_Dresden.JPG/340px-Fan-Tankstelle_von_Dynamo_Dresden.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fan-Tankstelle_von_Dynamo_Dresden.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Extensive use of the terminal digit 'nine' suggests that psychological pricing is at play</div></div></div>
<p>The broad <a href="/wiki/Pricing_strategies" title="Pricing strategies">pricing strategy</a> is normally established in the company's overall strategic plan. In the case of chain stores, the pricing strategy would be set by head office. Broadly, there are six approaches to pricing strategy mentioned in the marketing literature: operations-oriented,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dibb,_S._2013_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dibb,_S._2013-40">[40]</a></sup> revenue-oriented,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dibb,_S._2013_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dibb,_S._2013-40">[40]</a></sup> customer-oriented,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dibb,_S._2013_40-2"><a href="#cite_note-Dibb,_S._2013-40">[40]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Value-based_pricing" title="Value-based pricing">value-based</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Relationship-based_pricing" title="Relationship-based pricing">relationship-oriented</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> and socially-oriented.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> When decision-makers have determined the broad approach to pricing (i.e., the pricing strategy), they turn their attention to pricing tactics. Tactical pricing decisions are shorter term prices, designed to accomplish specific short-term goals. Pricing tactics that are commonly used in retail include discount pricing,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rao,_V.R_2009,_p.15_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rao,_V.R_2009,_p.15-45">[45]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Everyday_low_price" title="Everyday low price">everyday low prices</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hoch_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-hoch-46">[46]</a></sup> high-low pricing,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hoch_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-hoch-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Loss_leader" title="Loss leader">loss leaders</a>, <a href="/wiki/Product_bundling" title="Product bundling">product bundling</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> promotional pricing, and <a href="/wiki/Psychological_pricing" title="Psychological pricing">psychological pricing</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Retailers must also plan for customer preferred payment modes – e.g. cash, credit, lay-by, Electronic Funds Transfer at Point-of-Sale (EFTPOS). All payment options require some type of handling and attract costs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Contrary to <a href="/wiki/List_of_common_misconceptions" title="List of common misconceptions">common misconception</a>, price is not the most important factor for consumers, when deciding to buy a product.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mcdonald%27s_-_panoramio.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3672" data-file-width="4896" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Mcdonald%27s_-_panoramio.jpg/220px-Mcdonald%27s_-_panoramio.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Mcdonald%27s_-_panoramio.jpg/330px-Mcdonald%27s_-_panoramio.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Mcdonald%27s_-_panoramio.jpg/440px-Mcdonald%27s_-_panoramio.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mcdonald%27s_-_panoramio.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>One of the most well-known cross-selling sales scripts comes from McDonald's. "Would you like fries with that?"</div></div></div>
<p>Because patronage at a retail outlet varies, flexibility in <a href="/wiki/Schedule_(workplace)" title="Schedule (workplace)">scheduling</a> is desirable. <a href="/wiki/Employee_scheduling_software" title="Employee scheduling software">Employee scheduling software</a> is sold, which, using known patterns of customer patronage, more or less reliably predicts the need for staffing for various functions at times of the year, day of the month or week, and time of day. Usually needs vary widely. Conforming staff utilization to staffing needs requires a flexible workforce which is available when needed but does not have to be paid when they are not, <a href="/wiki/Part-time_job" title="Part-time job">part-time</a> <a href="/wiki/Workforce" title="Workforce">workers</a>; as of 2012 70% of retail workers in the United States were part-time. This may result in financial problems for the workers, who while they are required to be available at all times if their work hours are to be maximized, may not have sufficient income to meet their family and other obligations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT102812_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT102812-52">[52]</a></sup>  Retailers can employ different techniques to enhance sales volume and to improve the customer experience, such as Add-on, Upsell or Cross-sell; Selling on value;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> and knowing when to close the sale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>Transactional marketing aims to find target consumers, then negotiate, trade, and finally end relationships to complete the transaction. In this one-time transaction process, both parties aim to maximize their own interests. As a result, transactional marketing raises follow-up problems such as poor after-sales service quality and a lack of feedback channels for both parties. In addition, because retail enterprises needed to redevelop client relationships for each transaction, marketing costs were high and customer retention was low. All these downsides to transactional marketing gradually pushed the retail industry towards establishing long-term cooperative relationships with customers. Through this lens, enterprises began to focus on the process from transaction to relationship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
While expanding the sales market and attracting new customers is very important for the retail industry, it is also important to establish and maintain long term good relationships with previous customers, hence the name of the underlying concept, "relational marketing". Under this concept, retail enterprises value and attempt to improve relationships with customers, as customer relationships are conducive to maintaining stability in the current competitive retail market, and are also the future of retail enterprises.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Servicescapes.pdf"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1239" data-file-width="1754" decoding="async" height="233" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Servicescapes.pdf/page1-330px-Servicescapes.pdf.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Servicescapes.pdf/page1-495px-Servicescapes.pdf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Servicescapes.pdf/page1-660px-Servicescapes.pdf.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Servicescapes.pdf" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Simplified servicescapes model</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Change_the_way_you_WiFi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="811" data-file-width="1568" decoding="async" height="114" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Change_the_way_you_WiFi.jpg/220px-Change_the_way_you_WiFi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Change_the_way_you_WiFi.jpg/330px-Change_the_way_you_WiFi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Change_the_way_you_WiFi.jpg/440px-Change_the_way_you_WiFi.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Change_the_way_you_WiFi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Modern technologies are often displayed in clean environments with much empty space.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Harrods_Food_Department_Halls_(8473364801).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="745" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Harrods_Food_Department_Halls_%288473364801%29.jpg/220px-Harrods_Food_Department_Halls_%288473364801%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Harrods_Food_Department_Halls_%288473364801%29.jpg/330px-Harrods_Food_Department_Halls_%288473364801%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Harrods_Food_Department_Halls_%288473364801%29.jpg/440px-Harrods_Food_Department_Halls_%288473364801%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Harrods_Food_Department_Halls_(8473364801).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The retail servicescape includes the appearance, equipment, display space, retail counters, signage, layout and functionality of a retail outlet. Pictured: <a href="/wiki/Harrods" title="Harrods">Harrods</a> food court</div></div></div>
<p>Presentation refers to the physical evidence that signals the retail image. Physical evidence may include a diverse range of elements – the store itself including premises, offices, exterior facade and interior layout, websites, delivery vans, warehouses, staff uniforms. The environment in which the retail service encounter occurs is sometimes known as the <i>retail <a href="/wiki/Servicescape" title="Servicescape">servicescape</a>.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> The store environment consists of many elements such as smells, the physical environment (furnishings, layout and functionality), ambient conditions (lighting, temperature, noise) as well as signs, symbols and artifacts (e.g. sales promotions, shelf space, sample stations, visual communications). Retail designers pay close attention to the front of the store, which is known as the <i>decompression zone</i>. In order to maximise the number of selling opportunities, retailers generally want customers to spend more time in a retail store. However, this must be balanced against customer expectations surrounding convenience, access and realistic waiting times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> The way that brands are displayed is also part of the overall retail design. Where a product is placed on the shelves has implications for purchase likelihood as a result of visibility and access.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Ambient conditions, such as lighting, temperature and music, are also part of the overall retail environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bailey,_P._2015_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bailey,_P._2015-59">[59]</a></sup> It is common for a retail store to play music that relates to their target market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hul_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-hul-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<div id="Kinds_of_retailers"> </div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Shopper_profiles">Shopper profiles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Retail&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Shopper profiles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Two different strands of research have investigated shopper behaviour. One is primarily concerned with shopper motivations. The other stream of research seeks to segment shoppers according to common, shared characteristics. To some extent, these streams of research are inter-related, but each stream offers different types of insights into shopper behaviour.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Embroidery_and_lace-_their_manufacture_and_history_from_the_remotest_antiquity_to_the_present_day._A_handbook_for_amateurs,_collectors_and_general_readers_(1888)_(14593807460).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2404" data-file-width="1300" decoding="async" height="314" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Embroidery_and_lace-_their_manufacture_and_history_from_the_remotest_antiquity_to_the_present_day._A_handbook_for_amateurs%2C_collectors_and_general_readers_%281888%29_%2814593807460%29.jpg/170px-thumbnail.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Embroidery_and_lace-_their_manufacture_and_history_from_the_remotest_antiquity_to_the_present_day._A_handbook_for_amateurs%2C_collectors_and_general_readers_%281888%29_%2814593807460%29.jpg/255px-thumbnail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Embroidery_and_lace-_their_manufacture_and_history_from_the_remotest_antiquity_to_the_present_day._A_handbook_for_amateurs%2C_collectors_and_general_readers_%281888%29_%2814593807460%29.jpg/340px-thumbnail.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Embroidery_and_lace-_their_manufacture_and_history_from_the_remotest_antiquity_to_the_present_day._A_handbook_for_amateurs,_collectors_and_general_readers_(1888)_(14593807460).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>People who shop for pleasure are known as recreational shoppers. The recreational shopper has its origins in the grand European shopping arcades. Pictured: The gentry in a Dutch lace shop in the 17th century</div></div></div>
<p>Babin et al. carried out some of the earliest investigations into shopper motivations and identified two broad motives: <i>utilitarian</i> and <i>hedonic.</i> Utilitarian motivations are task-related and rational. For the shopper with utilitarian motives, purchasing is a work-related task that is to be accomplished in the most efficient and expedient manner. On the other hand, hedonic motives refer to pleasure. The shopper with hedonic motivations views shopping as a form of escapism where they are free to indulge fantasy and freedom. Hedonic shoppers are more involved in the shopping experience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many different shopper profiles can be identified. Retailers develop customised segmentation analyses for each unique outlet. However, it is possible to identify a number of broad shopper profiles. One of the most well-known and widely cited shopper typologies is that developed by Sproles and Kendal in the mid-1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Sproles and Kendall's consumer typology has been shown to be relatively consistent across time and across cultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Their typology is based on the consumer's approach to making purchase decisions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><b>Quality conscious/Perfectionist</b>: Quality-consciousness is characterised by a consumer's search for the very best quality in products; quality conscious consumers tend to shop systematically making more comparisons and shopping around.</li>
<li><b>Brand-conscious</b>: Brand-consciousness is characterised by a tendency to buy expensive, well-known brands or designer labels. Those who score high on brand-consciousness tend to believe that the higher prices are an indicator of quality and exhibit a preference for department stores or top-tier retail outlets.</li>
<li><b>Recreation-conscious/Hedonistic</b>: Recreational shopping is characterised by the consumer's engagement in the purchase process. Those who score high on recreation-consciousness regard shopping itself as a form of enjoyment.</li>
<li><b>Price-conscious</b>: A consumer who exhibits price-and-value consciousness. Price-conscious shoppers carefully shop around seeking lower prices, sales or discounts and are motivated by obtaining the best value for money</li>
<li><b>Novelty/fashion-conscious</b>: characterised by a consumer's tendency to seek out new products or new experiences for the sake of excitement; who gain excitement from seeking new things; they like to keep up-to-date with fashions and trends, variety-seeking is associated with this dimension.</li>
<li><b>Impulsive</b>: Impulsive consumers are somewhat careless in making purchase decisions, buy on the spur of the moment and are not overly concerned with expenditure levels or obtaining value. Those who score high on impulsive dimensions tend not to be engaged with the object at either a cognitive or emotional level.</li>
<li><b>Confused (by <a href="/wiki/Overchoice" title="Overchoice">overchoice</a>)</b>: characterised by a consumer's confusion caused by too many product choices, too many stores or an overload of product information; tend to experience information overload.</li>
<li><b>Habitual/brand loyal</b>: characterised by a consumer's tendency to follow a routine purchase pattern on each purchase occasion; consumers have favourite brands or stores and have formed habits in choosing; the purchase decision does not involve much evaluation or shopping around.</li></ul>
<p>Some researchers have adapted Sproles and Kendall's methodology for use in specific countries or cultural groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Consumer decision styles are important for retailers and marketers because they describe behaviours that are relatively stable over time and for this reason, they are useful for market segmentation.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types_of_retail_outlets"><span class="anchor" id="retail_format"></span><span class="anchor" id="retail_outlet"></span><span class="anchor" id="retail_shop"></span><span class="anchor" id="retail_store"></span>Types of retail outlets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Retail&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Types of retail outlets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Retail_format" title="Retail format">Retail format</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pfficeworks_inside.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4477" data-file-width="6549" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Pfficeworks_inside.jpg/220px-Pfficeworks_inside.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Pfficeworks_inside.jpg/330px-Pfficeworks_inside.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Pfficeworks_inside.jpg/440px-Pfficeworks_inside.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pfficeworks_inside.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Australia's <a href="/wiki/Officeworks" title="Officeworks">Officeworks</a> is a category killer, retailing everything for the home office or small commercial office; stationery, furniture, electronics, communications devices, copying, printing and photography services, coffee, tea and light snacks</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apple_Store,_Op%C3%A9ra_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Apple_Store%2C_Op%C3%A9ra_1.jpg/220px-Apple_Store%2C_Op%C3%A9ra_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Apple_Store%2C_Op%C3%A9ra_1.jpg/330px-Apple_Store%2C_Op%C3%A9ra_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Apple_Store%2C_Op%C3%A9ra_1.jpg/440px-Apple_Store%2C_Op%C3%A9ra_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apple_Store,_Op%C3%A9ra_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Apple's concept stores include video walls, Wi-Fi and desks to provide an immersive customer experience</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ScarsdaleGeneralStore.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2272" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/ScarsdaleGeneralStore.JPG/220px-ScarsdaleGeneralStore.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/ScarsdaleGeneralStore.JPG/330px-ScarsdaleGeneralStore.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/ScarsdaleGeneralStore.JPG/440px-ScarsdaleGeneralStore.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ScarsdaleGeneralStore.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A general store in Scarsdale, Victoria, Australia operates as a post-office, newsagent, petrol station, video hire, grocer and take-away food retailer</div></div></div><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Luovon_puoji.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1504" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Luovon_puoji.jpg/220px-Luovon_puoji.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Luovon_puoji.jpg/330px-Luovon_puoji.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Luovon_puoji.jpg/440px-Luovon_puoji.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Luovon_puoji.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A local store named "Luovon puoji" in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hailuoto_Island" title="Hailuoto Island">Hailuoto Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a></div></div></div><p><i>Retail formats</i> (also known as <i>retail formulas</i>) influence the consumer's store choice and addresses the consumer's expectations. At its most basic level, a retail format is a simple <a href="/wiki/Marketplace" title="Marketplace">marketplace</a>, that is; a location where goods and services are exchanged. In some parts of the world, the retail sector is still dominated by small family-run stores, but large <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retail_chains" title="Retail chains">retail chains</a> are increasingly dominating the sector, because they can exert considerable buying power and pass on the savings in the form of lower prices. Many of these large retail chains also produce their own private labels which compete alongside manufacturer brands. Considerable consolidation of retail stores has changed the retail landscape, transferring power away from wholesalers and into the hands of the large retail chains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>  In Britain and Europe, the retail sale of goods is designated as a <i><a href="/wiki/Services_marketing" title="Services marketing">service activity</a>.</i> The European Service Directive applies to all retail trade including periodic markets, street traders and peddlers.
</p><p>Retail stores may be classified by the type of product carried. Softline retailers sell goods that are consumed after a <a href="/wiki/Disposable_product" title="Disposable product">single-use</a>, or have a limited life (typically under three years) in they are normally consumed. Soft goods include <a href="/wiki/Clothing" title="Clothing">clothing</a>, other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fabrics" title="Fabrics">fabrics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Footwear" title="Footwear">footwear</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toiletries" title="Toiletries">toiletries</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cosmetics" title="Cosmetics">cosmetics</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medicines" title="Medicines">medicines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stationery" title="Stationery">stationery</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Grocery_store" title="Grocery store">Grocery stores</a>, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supermarkets" title="Supermarkets">supermarkets</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypermarkets" title="Hypermarkets">hypermarkets</a>, along with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Convenience_stores" title="Convenience stores">convenience stores</a> carry a mix of food products and consumable household items such as detergents, cleansers, personal hygiene products. Retailers selling consumer durables are sometimes known as <i>hardline retailers</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Automobiles" title="Automobiles">automobiles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Home_appliance" title="Home appliance">appliances</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electronics" title="Electronics">electronics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Furniture" title="Furniture">furniture</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sporting_goods" title="Sporting goods">sporting goods</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lumber" title="Lumber">lumber</a>, etc., and parts for them. Specialist retailers operate in many industries such as the arts e.g. green grocers, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Contemporary_art_galleries" title="Contemporary art galleries">contemporary art galleries</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bookstores" title="Bookstores">bookstores</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Handicrafts" title="Handicrafts">handicrafts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Music_store" title="Music store">musical instruments</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gift_shops" title="Gift shops">gift shops</a>.
</p><p>Types of retail outlets by marketing strategy include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shopping_arcade" title="Shopping arcade">shopping arcade</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anchor_tenant" title="Anchor tenant">anchor store</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bazaar" title="Bazaar">bazaar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boutique" title="Boutique">boutique</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Category_killer" title="Category killer">category killer</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Chain_store" title="Chain store">chain store</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Consumers%27_co-operative" title="Consumers' co-operative">co-operative store</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Convenience_store" title="Convenience store">convenience store</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Department_store" title="Department store">department stores</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Discount_store" title="Discount store">discount stores</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/E-tailer" title="E-tailer">e-tailer</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/General_store" title="General store">general store</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Give-away_shop" title="Give-away shop">give-away shop</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hawker_(trade)" title="Hawker (trade)">hawkers</a> also known as <a href="/wiki/Peddler" title="Peddler">peddlers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Costermonger" title="Costermonger">costermongers</a> or street vendors,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/High_Street" title="High Street">high street</a> store,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hypermarket" title="Hypermarket">hypermarket</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pop-up_retail" title="Pop-up retail">pop-up retail</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Marketplace" title="Marketplace">marketplace</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Market_square" title="Market square">market square</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shopping_center" title="Shopping center">shopping center</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> speciality store,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-essential_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-essential-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Supermarket" title="Supermarket">supermarket</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Variety_store" title="Variety store">variety stores</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vending_machine" title="Vending machine">vending machine</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/No_frills" title="No frills">no frills</a>, <a href="/wiki/Warehouse_club" title="Warehouse club">warehouse clubs</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Warehouse_store" title="Warehouse store">warehouse stores</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Automated_Retail" title="Automated Retail">automated retail</a>, <a href="/wiki/Big-box_store" title="Big-box store">big-box stores</a>, <a href="/wiki/Second-hand_shop" title="Second-hand shop">second-hand shop</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Charity_shop" title="Charity shop">charity shop</a>. Retailers can opt for a format as each provides different retail mix to its customers based on their customer demographics, lifestyle and purchase behavior. An effective format will determine how products are display products, as well as how target customers are attracted.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Challenges">Challenges</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Retail&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Challenges">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>To achieve and maintain a foothold in an existing market, a prospective retail establishment must <a href="/wiki/Barriers_to_entry" title="Barriers to entry">overcome the following hurdles</a>:
</p>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Regulations" title="Regulations">regulatory</a> barriers including:
<ul><li>restrictions on real-estate purchases, especially as imposed by <a href="/wiki/Municipality" title="Municipality">local governments</a> and against "big-box" <a href="/wiki/Big-box_store" title="Big-box store">chain retailers</a></li>
<li>restrictions on foreign investment in retailers, in terms of both absolute amount of financing provided and percentage share of voting stock (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Common_stock" title="Common stock">common stock</a>) purchased</li></ul></li>
<li>unfavorable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tax_code" title="Tax code">taxation structures</a>, especially those designed to penalize or keep out "big box" retailers (see "Regulatory" above)</li>
<li>absence of developed supply-chain and integrated IT management</li>
<li>high <a href="/wiki/Competition_(economics)" title="Competition (economics)">competitiveness</a> among existing market participants and resulting low <a href="/wiki/Profit_margin" title="Profit margin">profit margins</a>, caused in part by:
<ul><li>constant advances in product design resulting in constant threat of product obsolescence and price declines for existing <a href="/wiki/Inventory" title="Inventory">inventory</a></li></ul></li>
<li>lack of a properly-educated and/or -trained work-force, often including management, caused in part by loss in business<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (September 2020)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
<ul><li>lack of <a href="/wiki/Education_in_the_United_States" title="Education in the United States">educational infrastructure</a> enabling prospective market entrants to respond to the above challenges</li></ul></li>
<li>direct <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/E-tailing" title="E-tailing">e-tailing</a> (for example, through the <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">Internet</a>) and direct delivery to consumers from manufacturers and suppliers, cutting out any retail <a href="/wiki/Reseller" title="Reseller">middle man</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Method">Method</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Retail&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Method">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>When discussing the impact of technology on shopping and retail, e-commerce is often the first thing that comes to mind for retailers. However, technologies such as big data, artificial intelligence, computer vision and the Internet of Things have used data to transform every part of the shopping experience, from browsing to checkout.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p>It is important for organizations to embrace digital disruption in order to gain a competitive advantage. When an industry experiences digital disruption, it typically signals that consumer needs are shifting. Retailers enhance their analytics process and make better informed decisions thanks to big data, artificial intelligence, computer vision, and the Internet of Things. The use of data by retailers is mostly evident in the following aspects, based on the above-mentioned new technologies:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Enhance marketing by Personalizing customer experience</li>
<li>Optimize supply chain management</li>
<li>Adjust prices to maximize profits</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Consolidation">Consolidation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Retail&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Consolidation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Among retailers and retails chains a lot of consolidation has appeared over the last couple of decades. Between 1988 and 2010, worldwide 40,788 <a href="/wiki/Mergers_and_acquisitions" title="Mergers and acquisitions">mergers &amp; acquisitions</a> with a total known value of US$2.255 trillion have been announced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> The largest transactions with involvement of retailers in/from the United States have been: the acquisition of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Albertsons_(SuperValu)" title="Albertsons (SuperValu)">Albertson's Inc.</a> for US$17 billion in 2006,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> the merger between Federated Department Stores Inc with <a href="/wiki/The_May_Department_Stores_Company" title="The May Department Stores Company">May Department Stores</a> valued at 16.5 bil. USD in 2005<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> – now <a href="/wiki/Macy%27s,_Inc." title="Macy's, Inc.">Macy's</a>, and the merger between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kmart_(United_States)" title="Kmart (United States)">Kmart Holding Corp</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sears" title="Sears">Sears Roebuck &amp; Co</a> with a value of US$10.9 billion in 2004.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>Between 1985 and 2018 there have been 46,755 mergers or acquisitions conducted globally in the retail sector (either acquirer or target from the retail industry). These deals cumulate to an overall known value of around US$2,561 billion. The three major Retail M&amp;A waves took place in 2000, 2007 and lately in 2017. However the all-time high in terms of number of deals was in 2016 with more than 2,700 deals. In terms of added value 2007 set the record with the US$225 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>Here is a list of the top ten largest deals (ranked by volume) in the Retail Industry:<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Statistics">Statistics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Retail&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Statistics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Global_top_ten_retailers">Global top ten retailers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Retail&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Global top ten retailers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<p>As of 2016, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> was the largest retail market in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-www.emarketer.com_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-www.emarketer.com-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Competition">Competition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Retail&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Competition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Retail stores may or may not have competitors close enough to affect their pricing, product availability, and other operations. A 2006 survey found that only 38% of retail stores in India believed they faced more than slight competition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-retailcomp1-a_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-retailcomp1-a-109">[109]</a></sup> Competition also affected less than half of retail stores in Kazakhstan, Bulgaria, and Azerbaijan. In all countries the main competition was domestic, not foreign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-retailcomp30-a_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-retailcomp30-a-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p>
